<img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="326" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/10px-Socrates.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/15px-Socrates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/21px-Socrates.png 2x" width="10"/> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Philosophy" title="Portal:Philosophy">Philosophy portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Philosophy_sidebar" title="Template:Philosophy sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Philosophy_sidebar" title="Template talk:Philosophy sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Philosophy_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Ethics</b> or <b>moral philosophy</b> is a branch<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup> of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that "involves systematizing, defending, and recommending <a href="/wiki/Concept" title="Concept">concepts</a> of <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">right</a> and wrong <a href="/wiki/Action_(philosophy)" title="Action (philosophy)">behavior</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iep.utm.edu_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-iep.utm.edu-2">[2]</a></sup> The field of ethics, along with <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">aesthetics</a>, concerns matters of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Value_(ethics)" title="Value (ethics)">value</a>; these fields comprise the branch of philosophy called <a href="/wiki/Axiology" title="Axiology">axiology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ethics seeks to resolve questions of human <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a> by defining concepts such as <a href="/wiki/Good_and_evil" title="Good and evil">good and evil</a>, right and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wrong" title="Wrong">wrong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virtue" title="Virtue">virtue</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vice" title="Vice">vice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Justice" title="Justice">justice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crime" title="Crime">crime</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> As a field of <a href="/wiki/Intellectual" title="Intellectual">intellectual</a> inquiry, moral philosophy is related to the fields of <a href="/wiki/Moral_psychology" title="Moral psychology">moral psychology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Descriptive_ethics" title="Descriptive ethics">descriptive ethics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Value_theory" title="Value theory">value theory</a>.
</p><p>Three major areas of study within ethics recognized today are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iep.utm.edu_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-iep.utm.edu-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Meta-ethics" title="Meta-ethics">Meta-ethics</a>, concerning the theoretical meaning and reference of moral propositions, and how their <a href="/wiki/Truth_value" title="Truth value">truth values</a> (if any) can be determined;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Normative_ethics" title="Normative ethics">Normative ethics</a>, concerning the practical means of determining a moral course of action;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Applied_ethics" title="Applied ethics">Applied ethics</a>, concerning what a person is <a href="/wiki/Obligation" title="Obligation">obligated</a> (or permitted) to do in a specific situation or a particular domain of action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iep.utm.edu_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-iep.utm.edu-2">[2]</a></sup></li></ol>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Defining_ethics">Defining ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Defining ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The English word <i>ethics</i> is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> word <i>ēthikós</i> (<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ἠθικός</span></span>), meaning "relating to one's character", which itself comes from the root word <i>êthos</i> (<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ἦθος</span></span>) meaning "character, moral nature".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> This word was transferred into <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> as <i>ethica</i> and then into French as <i>éthique</i>, from which it was transferred into English.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Rushworth_Kidder" title="Rushworth Kidder">Rushworth Kidder</a> states that "standard definitions of <i>ethics</i> have typically included such phrases as 'the science of the ideal human character' or 'the science of moral duty'<span style="padding-left:.15em;">"</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Richard William Paul and <a href="/wiki/Linda_Elder" title="Linda Elder">Linda Elder</a> define ethics as "a set of concepts and principles that guide us in determining what behavior helps or harms sentient creatures".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reference_A_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reference_A-7">[7]</a></sup> The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cambridge_Dictionary_of_Philosophy" title="Cambridge Dictionary of Philosophy">Cambridge Dictionary of Philosophy</a></i> states that the word "ethics" is "commonly used interchangeably with '<a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a>' ... and sometimes it is used more narrowly to mean the moral principles of a particular tradition, group or individual."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Paul and Elder state that most people confuse ethics with behaving in accordance with social conventions, religious beliefs, the law, and do not treat ethics as a stand-alone concept.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>The word <i>ethics</i> in English refers to several things.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> It can refer to philosophical ethics or moral philosophy—a project that attempts to use reason to answer various kinds of ethical questions. As the English moral philosopher <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Williams" title="Bernard Williams">Bernard Williams</a> writes, attempting to explain moral philosophy: "What makes an inquiry a philosophical one is reflective generality and a style of argument that claims to be rationally persuasive."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Williams describes the content of this area of inquiry as addressing the very broad question, "how one should live".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Ethics can also refer to a common human ability to think about ethical problems that is not particular to philosophy. As <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bioethicist" title="Bioethicist">bioethicist</a> Larry Churchill has written: "Ethics, understood as the capacity to think critically about moral values and direct our actions in terms of such <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Value_(ethics)" title="Value (ethics)">values</a>, is a generic human capacity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Meta-ethics">Meta-ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Meta-ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Meta-ethics" title="Meta-ethics">Meta-ethics</a></div>
<p>Meta-ethics is the branch of philosophical ethics that asks how we understand, know about, and what we mean when we talk about what is right and what is wrong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-14">[14]</a></sup> An ethical question pertaining to a particular practical situation—such as, "Should I eat this particular piece of chocolate cake?"—cannot be a meta-ethical question (rather, this is an applied ethical question). A meta-ethical question is abstract and relates to a wide range of more specific practical questions. For example, "Is it ever possible to have a secure knowledge of what is right and wrong?" is a meta-ethical question.
</p><p>Meta-ethics has always accompanied philosophical ethics. For example, Aristotle implies that less precise knowledge is possible in ethics than in other spheres of inquiry, and he regards ethical knowledge as depending upon habit and <a href="/wiki/Acculturation" title="Acculturation">acculturation</a> in a way that makes it distinctive from other kinds of knowledge. Meta-ethics is also important in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Edward_Moore" title="George Edward Moore">G.E. Moore</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Principia_Ethica" title="Principia Ethica">Principia Ethica</a></i> from 1903. In it he first wrote about what he called <i>the <a href="/wiki/Naturalistic_fallacy" title="Naturalistic fallacy">naturalistic fallacy</a></i>. Moore was seen to reject <a href="/wiki/Ethical_naturalism" title="Ethical naturalism">naturalism</a> in ethics, in his <a href="/wiki/Open-question_argument" title="Open-question argument">open-question argument</a>. This made thinkers look again at second order questions about ethics. Earlier, the Scottish <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosopher</a> <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> had put forward a similar view on the difference between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Is-ought_problem" title="Is-ought problem">facts and values</a>.
</p><p>Studies of how we know in ethics divide into <a href="/wiki/Cognitivism_(ethics)" title="Cognitivism (ethics)">cognitivism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Non-cognitivism" title="Non-cognitivism">non-cognitivism</a>; these, respectively, take descriptive and non-descriptive approaches to moral goodness or value. Non-cognitivism is the view that when we judge something as morally right or wrong, this is neither true nor false. We may, for example, be only expressing our emotional feelings about these things.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Cognitivism can then be seen as the claim that when we talk about right and wrong, we are talking about matters of fact.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">ontology</a> of ethics is about <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Value_(ethics)" title="Value (ethics)">value</a>-bearing things or properties, that is, the kind of things or stuff referred to by ethical propositions. Non-descriptivists and non-cognitivists believe that ethics does not need a specific ontology since ethical propositions do not refer. This is known as an anti-realist position. Realists, on the other hand, must explain what kind of entities, properties or states are relevant for ethics, how they have value, and why they guide and motivate our actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moral_skepticism">Moral skepticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Moral skepticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moral_skepticism" title="Moral skepticism">Moral skepticism</a></div>
<p>Moral skepticism (or moral scepticism) is a <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class (philosophy)">class</a> of <a href="/wiki/Meta-ethics" title="Meta-ethics">metaethical</a> theories in which all members entail that no one has any moral knowledge. Many moral skeptics also make the stronger, <a href="/wiki/Modal_logic" title="Modal logic">modal</a> claim that <a href="/wiki/Moral_nihilism" title="Moral nihilism">moral knowledge is impossible</a>. Moral skepticism is particularly against <a href="/wiki/Moral_realism" title="Moral realism">moral realism</a> which holds the view that there are knowable and objective moral truths.
</p><p>Some proponents of moral skepticism include <a href="/wiki/Pyrrho" title="Pyrrho">Pyrrho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aenesidemus" title="Aenesidemus">Aenesidemus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sextus_Empiricus" title="Sextus Empiricus">Sextus Empiricus</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>, <a href="/wiki/Max_Stirner" title="Max Stirner">Max Stirner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche</a>, and <a href="/wiki/J._L._Mackie" title="J. L. Mackie">J.L. Mackie</a>.
</p><p>Moral skepticism is divided into three sub-classes:
</p>
<ul><li>Moral error theory (or <a href="/wiki/Moral_nihilism" title="Moral nihilism">moral nihilism</a>).</li>
<li>Epistemological moral skepticism.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Non-cognitivism" title="Non-cognitivism">Non-cognitivism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>All of these three theories share the same conclusions, which are as follows:
</p>
<dl><dd>(a) we are never justified in believing that moral claims (claims of the form "state of affairs x is good," "action y is morally obligatory," etc.) are true and, even more so</dd>
<dd>(b) we never know that any moral claim is true.</dd></dl>
<p>However, each method arrives at (a) and (b) by different routes.
</p><p>Moral error theory holds that we do not know that any moral claim is true because
</p>
<dl><dd>(i) all moral claims are false,</dd>
<dd>(ii) we have reason to believe that all moral claims are false, and</dd>
<dd>(iii) since we are not justified in believing any claim we have reason to deny, we are not justified in believing any moral claims.</dd></dl>
<p>Epistemological moral skepticism is a subclass of theory, the members of which include <a href="/wiki/Pyrrhonism" title="Pyrrhonism">Pyrrhonian</a> moral skepticism and dogmatic moral skepticism. All members of epistemological moral skepticism share two things: first, they acknowledge that we are unjustified in believing any moral claim, and second, they are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agnostic" title="Agnostic">agnostic</a> on whether (i) is true (i.e. on whether all moral claims are false).
</p>
<ul><li>Pyrrhonian moral skepticism holds that the reason we are unjustified in believing any moral claim is that it is irrational for us to believe either that any moral claim is true or that any moral claim is false. Thus, in addition to being agnostic on whether (i) is true, Pyrrhonian moral skepticism denies (ii).</li>
<li>Dogmatic moral skepticism, on the other hand, <i>affirms</i> (ii) and cites (ii)'s truth as the reason we are unjustified in believing any moral claim.</li></ul>
<p>Noncognitivism holds that we can never know that any moral claim is true because moral claims are <i>incapable</i> of being true or false (they are not truth-apt). Instead, moral claims are <a href="/wiki/Universal_prescriptivism" title="Universal prescriptivism">imperatives</a> (e.g. "Don't steal babies!"), <a href="/wiki/Emotivism" title="Emotivism">expressions of emotion</a> (e.g. "stealing babies: Boo!"), or <a href="/wiki/Expressivism" title="Expressivism">expressions of "pro-attitudes"</a> ("I do not believe that babies should be stolen.")
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Normative_ethics">Normative ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Normative ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Normative_ethics" title="Normative ethics">Normative ethics</a></div>
<p>Normative ethics is the study of ethical action. It is the branch of ethics that investigates the set of questions that arise when considering how one ought to act, morally speaking. Normative ethics is distinct from <a href="/wiki/Meta-ethics" title="Meta-ethics">meta-ethics</a> because normative ethics examines standards for the rightness and wrongness of actions, while meta-ethics studies the meaning of moral language and the metaphysics of moral facts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-14">[14]</a></sup> Normative ethics is also distinct from <a href="/wiki/Descriptive_ethics" title="Descriptive ethics">descriptive ethics</a>, as the latter is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empirical" title="Empirical">empirical</a> investigation of people's moral beliefs. To put it another way, descriptive ethics would be concerned with determining what proportion of people believe that killing is always wrong, while normative ethics is concerned with whether it is correct to hold such a belief. Hence, normative ethics is sometimes called prescriptive rather than descriptive. However, on certain versions of the meta-ethical view called <a href="/wiki/Moral_realism" title="Moral realism">moral realism</a>, moral facts are both descriptive and prescriptive at the same time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditionally, normative ethics (also known as moral theory) was the study of what makes actions right and wrong. These theories offered an overarching moral principle one could appeal to in resolving difficult moral decisions.
</p><p>At the turn of the 20th century, moral theories became more complex and were no longer concerned solely with rightness and wrongness, but were interested in many different kinds of moral status. During the middle of the century, the study of normative ethics declined as meta-ethics grew in prominence. This focus on meta-ethics was in part caused by an intense linguistic focus in <a href="/wiki/Analytic_philosophy" title="Analytic philosophy">analytic philosophy</a> and by the popularity of <a href="/wiki/Logical_positivism" title="Logical positivism">logical positivism</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Virtue_ethics">Virtue ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Virtue ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Virtue_ethics" title="Virtue ethics">Virtue ethics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Socrates_BM_GR1973.03-27.16.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3810" data-file-width="2540" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Socrates_BM_GR1973.03-27.16.jpg/170px-Socrates_BM_GR1973.03-27.16.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Socrates_BM_GR1973.03-27.16.jpg/255px-Socrates_BM_GR1973.03-27.16.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Socrates_BM_GR1973.03-27.16.jpg/340px-Socrates_BM_GR1973.03-27.16.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Socrates_BM_GR1973.03-27.16.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a></div></div></div>
<p>Virtue ethics describes the character of a moral agent as a driving force for ethical behavior, and it is used to describe the ethics of early Greek philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, and ancient Indian philosophers such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valluvar" title="Valluvar">Valluvar</a>. <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> (469–399 BC) was one of the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_philosophy" title="Greek philosophy">Greek philosophers</a> to encourage both scholars and the common citizen to turn their attention from the outside world to the condition of humankind. In this view, <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> bearing on human life was placed highest, while all other knowledge was secondary. <a href="/wiki/Self-knowledge_(psychology)" title="Self-knowledge (psychology)">Self-knowledge</a> was considered necessary for success and inherently an essential good. A self-aware person will act completely within his capabilities to his pinnacle, while an ignorant person will flounder and encounter difficulty. To Socrates, a person must become aware of every fact (and its context) relevant to his existence, if he wishes to attain self-knowledge. He posited that people will naturally do what is good if they know what is right. Evil or bad actions are the results of ignorance. If a criminal was truly aware of the intellectual and spiritual consequences of his or her actions, he or she would neither commit nor even consider committing those actions. Any person who knows what is truly right will automatically do it, according to Socrates. While he correlated knowledge with <a href="/wiki/Virtue" title="Virtue">virtue</a>, he similarly equated virtue with <a href="/wiki/Joy" title="Joy">joy</a>. The truly wise man will know what is right, do what is good, and therefore be happy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahakianSahakian1966_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahakianSahakian1966-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 32–33">: 32–33 </span></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (384–323 BC) posited an ethical system that may be termed "virtuous". In Aristotle's view, when a person acts in accordance with virtue this person will do good and be content. Unhappiness and frustration are caused by doing wrong, leading to failed goals and a poor life. Therefore, it is imperative for people to act in accordance with virtue, which is only attainable by the practice of the virtues in order to be content and complete. Happiness was held to be the ultimate goal. All other things, such as civic life or <a href="/wiki/Wealth" title="Wealth">wealth</a>, were only made worthwhile and of benefit when employed in the practice of the virtues. The practice of the virtues is the surest path to happiness. Aristotle asserted that the soul of man had three natures<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Cannot find any sources to corroborate this claim (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>: body (physical/metabolism), animal (emotional/appetite), and rational (mental/conceptual). Physical nature can be assuaged through exercise and care; emotional nature through indulgence of instinct and urges; and mental nature through human reason and developed potential. Rational development was considered the most important, as essential to philosophical self-awareness, and as uniquely human. <a href="/wiki/Moderation" title="Moderation">Moderation</a> was encouraged, with the extremes seen as degraded and immoral. For example, <a href="/wiki/Courage" title="Courage">courage</a> is the moderate virtue between the extremes of <a href="/wiki/Cowardice" title="Cowardice">cowardice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Recklessness_(psychology)" title="Recklessness (psychology)">recklessness</a>. Man should not simply live, but live well with conduct governed by virtue. This is regarded as difficult, as virtue denotes doing the right thing, in the right way, at the right time, for the right reason.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valluvar" title="Valluvar">Valluvar</a> (before 5th century CE) keeps virtue, or <i>aṟam</i> (<a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">dharma</a>) as he calls it, as the cornerstone throughout the writing of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kural_literature" title="Kural literature">Kural literature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> While religious scriptures generally consider <i>aṟam</i> as divine in nature, Valluvar describes it as a way of life rather than any spiritual observance, a way of harmonious living that leads to universal happiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sanjeevi_SeminarPapers_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sanjeevi_SeminarPapers-21">[21]</a></sup> Contrary to what other contemporary works say, Valluvar holds that <i>aṟam</i> is common for all, irrespective of whether the person is a bearer of palanquin or the rider in it. Valluvar considered <a href="/wiki/Justice" title="Justice">justice</a> as a facet of <i>aṟam.</i> While <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">ancient Greek philosophers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, and their descendants opined that justice cannot be defined and that it was a divine mystery, Valluvar positively suggested that a divine origin is not required to define the concept of justice. In the words of <a href="/wiki/V._R._Nedunchezhiyan" title="V. R. Nedunchezhiyan">V. R. Nedunchezhiyan</a>, justice according to Valluvar "dwells in the minds of those who have knowledge of the standard of right and wrong; so too deceit dwells in the minds which breed fraud."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sanjeevi_SeminarPapers_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sanjeevi_SeminarPapers-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stoicism">Stoicism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Stoicism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">Stoicism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Epictetus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="346" data-file-width="273" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Epictetus.jpg/170px-Epictetus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Epictetus.jpg/255px-Epictetus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/90/Epictetus.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Epictetus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Epictetus" title="Epictetus">Epictetus</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">Stoic</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Epictetus" title="Epictetus">Epictetus</a> posited that the greatest good was contentment and serenity. Peace of mind, or <i><a href="/wiki/Apatheia" title="Apatheia">apatheia</a></i>, was of the highest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Value_(ethics)" title="Value (ethics)">value</a>; self-mastery over one's desires and emotions leads to spiritual peace. The "unconquerable will" is central to this philosophy. The individual's will should be independent and inviolate. Allowing a person to disturb the mental equilibrium is, in essence, offering yourself in slavery. If a person is free to anger you at will, you have no control over your internal world, and therefore no freedom. Freedom from material attachments is also necessary. If a thing breaks, the person should not be upset, but realize it was a thing that could break. Similarly, if someone should die, those close to them should hold to their serenity because the loved one was made of flesh and blood destined to death. Stoic philosophy says to accept things that cannot be changed, resigning oneself to the existence and enduring in a rational fashion. Death is not feared. People do not "lose" their life, but instead "return", for they are returning to God (who initially gave what the person is as a person). Epictetus said difficult problems in life should not be avoided, but rather embraced. They are spiritual exercises needed for the health of the spirit, just as physical exercise is required for the health of the body. He also stated that sex and sexual desire are to be avoided as the greatest threat to the <a href="/wiki/Integrity" title="Integrity">integrity</a> and equilibrium of a man's mind. Abstinence is highly desirable. Epictetus said remaining abstinent in the face of temptation was a victory for which a man could be proud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahakianSahakian1966_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-SahakianSahakian1966-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 38–41">: 38–41 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_virtue_ethics">Contemporary virtue ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Contemporary virtue ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Modern virtue ethics was popularized during the late 20th century in large part due to a revival of <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelianism</a>, and as a response to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G.E.M._Anscombe" title="G.E.M. Anscombe">G.E.M. Anscombe</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Modern_Moral_Philosophy" title="Modern Moral Philosophy">Modern Moral Philosophy</a>". Anscombe argues that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consequentialist" title="Consequentialist">consequentialist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deontological" title="Deontological">deontological</a> ethics are only feasible as universal theories if the two schools ground themselves in <a href="/wiki/Divine_law" title="Divine law">divine law</a>. As a deeply devoted Christian herself, Anscombe proposed that either those who do not give ethical credence to notions of divine law take up virtue ethics, which does not necessitate universal laws as agents themselves are investigated for virtue or vice and held up to "universal standards", or that those who wish to be utilitarian or consequentialist ground their theories in religious conviction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vries_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vries-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alasdair_MacIntyre" title="Alasdair MacIntyre">Alasdair MacIntyre</a>, who wrote the book <i><a href="/wiki/After_Virtue" title="After Virtue">After Virtue</a></i>, was a key contributor and proponent of modern virtue ethics, although some claim that MacIntyre supports a relativistic account of virtue based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norm_(social)" title="Norm (social)">cultural norms</a>, not objective standards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vries_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vries-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Martha_Nussbaum" title="Martha Nussbaum">Martha Nussbaum</a>, a contemporary virtue ethicist, objects to MacIntyre's relativism, among that of others, and responds to relativist objections to form an objective account in her work "Non-Relative Virtues: An Aristotelian Approach".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> However, Nussbaum's accusation of relativism appears to be a misreading. In <i>Whose Justice, Whose Rationality?</i>, MacIntyre's ambition of taking a rational path beyond relativism was quite clear when he stated "rival claims made by different traditions […] are to be evaluated […] without relativism" (p. 354) because indeed "rational debate between and rational choice among rival traditions is possible” (p. 352). <i>Complete Conduct Principles for the 21st Century</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethics_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-ethics-24">[24]</a></sup> blended the Eastern virtue ethics and the Western virtue ethics, with some modifications to suit the 21st Century, and formed a part of contemporary virtue ethics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethics_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-ethics-24">[24]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Mortimer_J._Adler" title="Mortimer J. Adler">Mortimer J. Adler</a> described Aristotle's <a href="/wiki/Nicomachean_Ethics" title="Nicomachean Ethics">Nicomachean Ethics</a> as a "unique book in the Western tradition of moral philosophy, the only ethics that is sound, practical, and undogmatic."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdler1985194_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdler1985194-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>One major trend in contemporary virtue ethics is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_Stoicism" title="Modern Stoicism">Modern Stoicism</a> movement.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intuitive_ethics">Intuitive ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Intuitive ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethical_intuitionism" title="Ethical intuitionism">Ethical intuitionism</a></div>
<p>Ethical intuitionism (also called moral intuitionism) is a family of views in moral <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> (and, on some definitions, <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>). At minimum, ethical intuitionism is the thesis that our <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intuition_(knowledge)" title="Intuition (knowledge)">intuitive awareness</a> of value, or intuitive knowledge of evaluative facts, forms the foundation of our ethical knowledge.
</p><p>The view is at its core a <a href="/wiki/Foundationalism" title="Foundationalism">foundationalism</a> about moral knowledge: it is the view that some moral truths can be known non-inferentially (i.e., known without one needing to infer them from other truths one believes). Such an epistemological view implies that there are moral beliefs with propositional contents; so it implies <a href="/wiki/Cognitivism_(ethics)" title="Cognitivism (ethics)">cognitivism</a>. As such, ethical intuitionism is to be contrasted with <a href="/wiki/Coherentism" title="Coherentism">coherentist</a> approaches to moral epistemology, such as those that depend on <a href="/wiki/Reflective_equilibrium" title="Reflective equilibrium">reflective equilibrium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shafer-Landau_&amp;_Cuneo_foundationalism_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shafer-Landau_&amp;_Cuneo_foundationalism-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout the philosophical literature, the term "ethical intuitionism" is frequently used with significant variation in its sense. This article's focus on foundationalism reflects the core commitments of contemporary self-identified ethical intuitionists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shafer-Landau_&amp;_Cuneo_foundationalism_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shafer-Landau_&amp;_Cuneo_foundationalism-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEP-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sufficiently broadly defined, ethical intuitionism can be taken to encompass cognitivist forms of <a href="/wiki/Moral_sense_theory" title="Moral sense theory">moral sense theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stratton-Lake_sentimentalism_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stratton-Lake_sentimentalism-28">[28]</a></sup> It is usually furthermore taken as essential to ethical intuitionism that there be <a href="/wiki/Self-evidence" title="Self-evidence">self-evident</a> or <i><a href="/wiki/A_priori_and_a_posteriori" title="A priori and a posteriori">a priori</a></i> moral knowledge; this counts against considering moral sense theory to be a species of intuitionism. (See the <a href="/wiki/Ethical_intuitionism#Rational_intuition_versus_moral_sense" title="Ethical intuitionism">Rational intuition versus moral sense</a> section of this article for further discussion.)
</p><p>Ethical intuitionism was first clearly shown in use by the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Francis_Hutcheson_(philosopher)" title="Francis Hutcheson (philosopher)">Francis Hutcheson</a>. Later ethical intuitionists of influence and note include <a href="/wiki/Henry_Sidgwick" title="Henry Sidgwick">Henry Sidgwick</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G.E._Moore" title="G.E. Moore">G.E. Moore</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harold_Arthur_Prichard" title="Harold Arthur Prichard">Harold Arthur Prichard</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C.S._Lewis" title="C.S. Lewis">C.S. Lewis</a> and, most influentially, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Audi" title="Robert Audi">Robert Audi</a>.
</p><p>Objections to ethical intuitionism include whether or not there are objective moral values (an assumption which the ethical system is based upon) the question of why many disagree over ethics if they are absolute, and whether <a href="/wiki/Occam%27s_razor" title="Occam's razor">Occam's razor</a> cancels such a theory out entirely.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hedonism">Hedonism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Hedonism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hedonism" title="Hedonism">Hedonism</a></div>
<p>Hedonism posits that the principal ethic is maximizing <a href="/wiki/Pleasure" title="Pleasure">pleasure</a> and minimizing <a href="/wiki/Suffering" title="Suffering">pain</a>. There are several schools of Hedonist thought ranging from those advocating the indulgence of even momentary desires to those teaching a pursuit of spiritual bliss. In their consideration of consequences, they range from those advocating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-gratification" title="Self-gratification">self-gratification</a> regardless of the pain and expense to others, to those stating that the most ethical pursuit maximizes pleasure and happiness for the most people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahakianSahakian1966_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-SahakianSahakian1966-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 37">: 37 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cyrenaic_hedonism">Cyrenaic hedonism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Cyrenaic hedonism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Founded by <a href="/wiki/Aristippus" title="Aristippus">Aristippus</a> of Cyrene, <a href="/wiki/Cyrenaics" title="Cyrenaics">Cyrenaics</a> supported immediate gratification or pleasure. "Eat, drink and be merry, for tomorrow we die." Even fleeting desires should be indulged, for fear the opportunity should be forever lost. There was little to no concern with the future, the present dominating in the pursuit of immediate pleasure. Cyrenaic hedonism encouraged the pursuit of enjoyment and indulgence without hesitation, believing pleasure to be the only good.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahakianSahakian1966_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-SahakianSahakian1966-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 37">: 37 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Epicureanism">Epicureanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Epicureanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Epicureanism" title="Epicureanism">Epicureanism</a></div>
<p>Epicurean ethics is a hedonist form of virtue ethics. <a href="/wiki/Epicurus" title="Epicurus">Epicurus</a> "presented a sustained argument that pleasure, correctly understood, will coincide with virtue."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> He rejected the extremism of the <a href="/wiki/Cyrenaics" title="Cyrenaics">Cyrenaics</a>, believing some pleasures and indulgences to be detrimental to human beings. <a href="/wiki/Epicureanism" title="Epicureanism">Epicureans</a> observed that indiscriminate indulgence sometimes resulted in negative consequences. Some experiences were therefore rejected out of hand, and some unpleasant experiences endured in the present to ensure a better life in the future. To Epicurus, the <i>summum bonum</i>, or greatest good, was prudence, exercised through moderation and caution. Excessive indulgence can be destructive to pleasure and can even lead to pain. For example, eating one food too often makes a person lose a taste for it. Eating too much food at once leads to discomfort and ill-health. Pain and fear were to be avoided. Living was essentially good, barring pain and illness. Death was not to be feared. Fear was considered the source of most unhappiness. Conquering the fear of death would naturally lead to a happier life. Epicurus reasoned if there were an afterlife and immortality, the fear of death was irrational. If there was no life after death, then the person would not be alive to suffer, fear, or worry; he would be non-existent in death. It is irrational to fret over circumstances that do not exist, such as one's state of death in the absence of an afterlife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahakianSahakian1966_19-4"><a href="#cite_note-SahakianSahakian1966-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 37–38">: 37–38 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_consequentialism">State consequentialism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: State consequentialism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/State_consequentialism" title="State consequentialism">State consequentialism</a></div>
<p>State consequentialism, also known as Mohist consequentialism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-readings_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-readings-30">[30]</a></sup> is an ethical theory that evaluates the moral worth of an action based on how much it contributes to the basic goods of a state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-readings_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-readings-30">[30]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Stanford_Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy" title="Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy">Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</a></i> describes Mohist consequentialism, dating back to the 5th century BC, as "a remarkably sophisticated version based on a plurality of intrinsic goods taken as constitutive of human welfare".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Unlike utilitarianism, which views pleasure as a moral good, "the basic goods in Mohist consequentialist thinking are … order, material wealth, and increase in population".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-32">[32]</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/Mozi" title="Mozi">Mozi</a>'s era, war and famines were common, and population growth was seen as a moral necessity for a harmonious society. The "material wealth" of Mohist consequentialism refers to <a href="/wiki/Maslow%27s_hierarchy_of_needs" title="Maslow's hierarchy of needs">basic needs</a> like shelter and clothing, and the "order" of Mohist consequentialism refers to Mozi's stance against warfare and violence, which he viewed as pointless and a threat to social stability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norden_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norden-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinologist" title="Sinologist">sinologist</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_S._Nivison" title="David S. Nivison">David Shepherd Nivison</a>, in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Cambridge_History_of_Ancient_China" title="The Cambridge History of Ancient China">The Cambridge History of Ancient China</a></i>, writes that the moral goods of Mohism "are interrelated: more basic wealth, then more reproduction; more people, then more production and wealth … if people have plenty, they would be good, filial, kind, and so on unproblematically."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-32">[32]</a></sup> The Mohists believed that morality is based on "promoting the benefit of all under heaven and eliminating harm to all under heaven". In contrast to Bentham's views, state consequentialism is not utilitarian because it is not hedonistic or individualistic. The importance of outcomes that are good for the community outweighs the importance of individual pleasure and pain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handbook_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Handbook-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consequentialism">Consequentialism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Consequentialism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Consequentialism" title="Consequentialism">Consequentialism</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ethical_egoism" title="Ethical egoism">Ethical egoism</a></div>
<p>Consequentialism refers to moral theories that hold the consequences of a particular action form the basis for any valid moral judgment about that action (or create a structure for judgment, see <a href="#Rule_consequentialism">rule consequentialism</a>). Thus, from a consequentialist standpoint, morally right action is one that produces a good outcome, or consequence. This view is often expressed as the <a href="/wiki/Aphorism" title="Aphorism">aphorism</a> <i>"The ends justify the means"</i>.
</p><p>The term "consequentialism" was coined by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G.E.M._Anscombe" title="G.E.M. Anscombe">G.E.M. Anscombe</a> in her essay "<a href="/wiki/Modern_Moral_Philosophy" title="Modern Moral Philosophy">Modern Moral Philosophy</a>" in 1958, to describe what she saw as the central error of certain moral theories, such as those propounded by <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">Mill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Sidgwick" title="Henry Sidgwick">Sidgwick</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anscombe_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anscombe-35">[35]</a></sup> Since then, the term has become common in English-language ethical theory.
</p><p>The defining feature of consequentialist moral theories is the weight given to the consequences in evaluating the rightness and wrongness of actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mackie_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mackie-36">[36]</a></sup> In consequentialist theories, the consequences of an action or rule generally outweigh other considerations. Apart from this basic outline, there is little else that can be unequivocally said about consequentialism as such. However, there are some questions that many consequentialist theories address:
</p>
<ul><li>What sort of consequences count as good consequences?</li>
<li>Who is the primary beneficiary of moral action?</li>
<li>How are the consequences judged and who judges them?</li></ul>
<p>One way to divide various consequentialisms is by the many types of consequences that are taken to matter most, that is, which consequences count as good states of affairs. According to <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarianism</a>, a good action is one that results in an increase and positive effect, and the best action is one that results in that effect for the greatest number. Closely related is <a href="/wiki/Eudaimonia" title="Eudaimonia">eudaimonic</a> consequentialism, according to which a full, flourishing life, which may or may not be the same as enjoying a great deal of pleasure, is the ultimate aim. Similarly, one might adopt an aesthetic consequentialism, in which the ultimate aim is to produce beauty. However, one might fix on non-psychological goods as the relevant effect. Thus, one might pursue an increase in <a href="/wiki/Equality_of_outcome" title="Equality of outcome">material equality</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedom_(political)" title="Freedom (political)">political liberty</a> instead of something like the more ephemeral "pleasure". Other theories adopt a package of several goods, all to be promoted equally. Whether a particular consequentialist theory focuses on a single good or many, conflicts and tensions between different good states of affairs are to be expected and must be adjudicated.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Utilitarianism">Utilitarianism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Utilitarianism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">Utilitarianism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="728" data-file-width="536" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg/170px-Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg/255px-Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg/340px-Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jeremy Bentham</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Stuart_Mill_by_London_Stereoscopic_Company,_c1870.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1274" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/John_Stuart_Mill_by_London_Stereoscopic_Company%2C_c1870.jpg/170px-John_Stuart_Mill_by_London_Stereoscopic_Company%2C_c1870.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/John_Stuart_Mill_by_London_Stereoscopic_Company%2C_c1870.jpg/255px-John_Stuart_Mill_by_London_Stereoscopic_Company%2C_c1870.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/John_Stuart_Mill_by_London_Stereoscopic_Company%2C_c1870.jpg/340px-John_Stuart_Mill_by_London_Stereoscopic_Company%2C_c1870.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Stuart_Mill_by_London_Stereoscopic_Company,_c1870.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>John Stuart Mill</div></div></div>
<p>Utilitarianism is an ethical theory that argues the proper course of action is one that maximizes a positive effect, such as "happiness", "welfare", or the ability to live according to personal preferences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Bentham" title="Jeremy Bentham">Jeremy Bentham</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a> are influential proponents of this school of thought. In <i>A Fragment on Government</i> Bentham says 'it is the greatest happiness of the greatest number that is the measure of right and wrong' and describes this as a fundamental <a href="/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">axiom</a>. In <i>An Introduction to the Principles of Morals and Legislation</i> he talks of 'the principle of utility' but later prefers "the greatest happiness principle".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Stuart_Mill_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-John_Stuart_Mill-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Utilitarianism is the paradigmatic example of a consequentialist moral theory. This form of utilitarianism holds that the morally correct action is the one that produces the best outcome for all people affected by the action. <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>, in his exposition of utilitarianism, proposed a hierarchy of pleasures, meaning that the pursuit of certain kinds of pleasure is more highly valued than the pursuit of other pleasures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mill_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mill-40">[40]</a></sup> Other noteworthy proponents of utilitarianism are neuroscientist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sam_Harris_(author)" title="Sam Harris (author)">Sam Harris</a>, author of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Moral_Landscape" title="The Moral Landscape">The Moral Landscape</a></i>, and moral philosopher <a href="/wiki/Peter_Singer" title="Peter Singer">Peter Singer</a>, author of, amongst other works, <i><a href="/wiki/Practical_Ethics" title="Practical Ethics">Practical Ethics</a></i>.
</p><p>The major division within utilitarianism is between <i><a href="/wiki/Act_utilitarianism" title="Act utilitarianism">act utilitarianism</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Rule_utilitarianism" title="Rule utilitarianism">rule utilitarianism</a></i>. In act utilitarianism, the principle of utility applies directly to each alternative act in a situation of choice. The right act is the one that brings about the best results (or the least bad results). In rule utilitarianism, the principle of utility determines the validity of rules of conduct (moral principles). A rule like promise-keeping is established by looking at the consequences of a world in which people break promises at will and a world in which promises are binding. Right and wrong are the following or breaking of rules that are sanctioned by their utilitarian value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carnegie_Mellon_University_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carnegie_Mellon_University-41">[41]</a></sup> A proposed "middle ground" between these two types is <a href="/wiki/Two-level_utilitarianism" title="Two-level utilitarianism">Two-level utilitarianism</a>, where rules are applied in ordinary circumstances, but with an allowance to choose actions outside of such rules when unusual situations call for it.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deontology">Deontology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Deontology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deontological_ethics" title="Deontological ethics">Deontological ethics</a></div>
<p>Deontological ethics or deontology (from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">δέον</span></span>, <i>deon</i>, "obligation, duty"; and <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">-λογία</span></span>, <i><a href="/wiki/-logy" title="-logy">-logia</a></i>) is an approach to ethics that determines goodness or rightness from examining acts, or the rules and duties that the person doing the act strove to fulfill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-42">[42]</a></sup> This is in contrast to <a href="/wiki/Consequentialism" title="Consequentialism">consequentialism</a>, in which rightness is based on the consequences of an act, and not the act by itself. Under deontology, an act may be considered right even if it produces a bad consequence,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> if it follows the <i>rule</i> or moral law. According to the deontological view, people have a <i>duty</i> to act in ways that are deemed inherently good ("truth-telling" for example), or follow an objectively obligatory rule (as in <a href="/wiki/Rule_utilitarianism" title="Rule utilitarianism">rule utilitarianism</a>).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kantianism">Kantianism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Kantianism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Immanuel_Kant_(painted_portrait).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1388" data-file-width="964" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Immanuel_Kant_%28painted_portrait%29.jpg/170px-Immanuel_Kant_%28painted_portrait%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Immanuel_Kant_%28painted_portrait%29.jpg/255px-Immanuel_Kant_%28painted_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Immanuel_Kant_%28painted_portrait%29.jpg/340px-Immanuel_Kant_%28painted_portrait%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Immanuel_Kant_(painted_portrait).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kantian_ethics" title="Kantian ethics">Kantian ethics</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a>'s theory of ethics is considered deontological for several different reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Orend,_Brian_2000_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Orend,_Brian_2000-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> First, Kant argues that to act in the morally right way, people must act from duty (<i>Pflicht</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Second, Kant argued that it was not the consequences of actions that make them right or wrong but the motives of the person who carries out the action.
</p><p>Kant's argument that to act in the morally right way one must act purely from duty begins with an argument that the highest good must be both good in itself and good without qualification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-transition_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-transition-47">[47]</a></sup> Something is "good in itself" when it is <a href="/wiki/Intrinsic_value_(ethics)" title="Intrinsic value (ethics)">intrinsically good</a>, and "good without qualification", when the addition of that thing never makes a situation ethically worse. Kant then argues that those things that are usually thought to be good, such as <a href="/wiki/Intelligence" title="Intelligence">intelligence</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/perseverance" title="wikt:perseverance">perseverance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pleasure" title="Pleasure">pleasure</a>, fail to be either intrinsically good or good without qualification. Pleasure, for example, appears not to be good without qualification, because when people take pleasure in watching someone suffer, this seems to make the situation ethically worse. He concludes that there is only one thing that is truly good:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Nothing in the world—indeed nothing even beyond the world—can possibly be conceived which could be called good without qualification except a <i>good will</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-transition_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-transition-47">[47]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>Kant then argues that the consequences of an act of willing cannot be used to determine that the person has a good will; good consequences could arise by accident from an action that was motivated by a desire to cause harm to an innocent person, and bad consequences could arise from an action that was well-motivated. Instead, he claims, a person has goodwill when he 'acts out of respect for the moral law'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-transition_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-transition-47">[47]</a></sup> People 'act out of respect for the moral law' when they act in some way <i>because</i> they have a duty to do so. So, the only thing that is truly good in itself is goodwill, and goodwill is only good when the willer chooses to do something because it is that person's duty, i.e. out of "respect" for the law. He defines respect as "the concept of a worth which thwarts my self-love".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Kant's three significant formulations of the <a href="/wiki/Categorical_imperative" title="Categorical imperative">categorical imperative</a> are:
</p>
<ul><li>Act only according to that <a href="/wiki/Maxim_(philosophy)" title="Maxim (philosophy)">maxim</a> by which you can also will that it would become a universal law.</li>
<li>Act in such a way that you always treat humanity, whether in your own person or in the person of any other, never simply as a means, but always at the same time as an end.</li>
<li>Every rational being must so act as if he were through his maxim always a legislating member in a universal kingdom of ends.</li></ul>
<p>Kant argued that the only <a href="/wiki/Moral_absolutism" title="Moral absolutism">absolutely good</a> thing is a good will, and so the single determining factor of whether an action is morally right is the will, or motive of the person doing it. If they are acting on a bad maxim, e.g. "I will lie", then their action is wrong, even if some good consequences come of it.
In his essay, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/On_a_Supposed_Right_to_Lie_Because_of_Philanthropic_Concerns" title="On a Supposed Right to Lie Because of Philanthropic Concerns">On a Supposed Right to Lie Because of Philanthropic Concerns</a></i>, arguing against the position of 
<a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Constant" title="Benjamin Constant">Benjamin Constant</a>, <i>Des réactions politiques</i>, Kant states that "Hence a lie defined merely as an intentionally untruthful declaration to another man does not require the additional condition that it must do harm to another, as jurists require in their definition (<i>mendacium est falsiloquium in praeiudicium alterius</i>). For a lie always harms another; if not some human being, then it nevertheless does harm to humanity in general, inasmuch as it vitiates the very source of right [<i>Rechtsquelle</i>] ... All practical principles of right must contain rigorous truth ... This is because such exceptions would destroy the universality on account of which alone they bear the name of principles."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Divine_command_theory">Divine command theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Divine command theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Divine_command_theory" title="Divine command theory">Divine command theory</a></div>
<p>Although not all deontologists are religious, some belief in the 'divine command theory', which is actually a cluster of related theories which essentially state that an action is right if God has decreed that it is right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Cudworth" title="Ralph Cudworth">Ralph Cudworth</a>, an English philosopher, <a href="/wiki/William_of_Ockham" title="William of Ockham">William of Ockham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>, and eighteenth-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calvinists" title="Calvinists">Calvinists</a> all accepted various versions of this moral theory, as they all held that moral obligations arise from God's commands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The Divine Command Theory is a form of deontology because, according to it, the rightness of any action depends upon that action being performed because it is a duty, not because of any good consequences arising from that action. If God commands people not to work on <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Sabbath" title="Biblical Sabbath">Sabbath</a>, then people act rightly if they do not work on Sabbath <i>because God has commanded that they do not do so</i>. If they do not work on Sabbath because they are lazy, then their action is not truly speaking "right", even though the actual physical action performed is the same. If God commands not to covet a neighbor's goods, this theory holds that it would be immoral to do so, even if coveting provides the beneficial outcome of a drive to succeed or do well.
</p><p>One thing that clearly distinguishes Kantian deontologism from divine command deontology is that Kantianism maintains that man, as a rational being, makes the moral law universal, whereas divine command maintains that God makes the moral law universal.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Discourse_ethics">Discourse ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Discourse ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JuergenHabermas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1959" data-file-width="2939" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/JuergenHabermas.jpg/220px-JuergenHabermas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/JuergenHabermas.jpg/330px-JuergenHabermas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/JuergenHabermas.jpg/440px-JuergenHabermas.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JuergenHabermas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photograph of Jurgen Habermas, whose theory of discourse ethics was influenced by Kantian ethics</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Discourse_ethics" title="Discourse ethics">Discourse ethics</a></div>
<p>German philosopher <a href="/wiki/J%C3%BCrgen_Habermas" title="Jürgen Habermas">Jürgen Habermas</a> has proposed a theory of <a href="/wiki/Discourse_ethics" title="Discourse ethics">discourse ethics</a> that he states is a descendant of Kantian ethics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> He proposes that action should be based on communication between those involved, in which their interests and intentions are discussed so they can be understood by all. Rejecting any form of coercion or manipulation, Habermas believes that agreement between the parties is crucial for a moral decision to be reached.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Like Kantian ethics, discourse ethics is a <a href="/wiki/Cognitivism_(ethics)" title="Cognitivism (ethics)">cognitive</a> ethical theory, in that it supposes that truth and falsity can be attributed to ethical propositions. It also formulates a rule by which ethical actions can be determined and proposes that ethical actions should be universalizable, in a similar way to Kant's ethics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shabani_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shabani-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Habermas argues that his ethical theory is an improvement on Kant's ethics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shabani_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shabani-54">[54]</a></sup> He rejects the dualistic framework of Kant's ethics. Kant distinguished between the <a href="/wiki/Phenomenon" title="Phenomenon">phenomena</a> world, which can be sensed and experienced by humans, and the <a href="/wiki/Noumenon" title="Noumenon">noumena</a>, or spiritual world, which is inaccessible to humans. This dichotomy was necessary for Kant because it could explain the autonomy of a human agent: although a human is bound in the phenomenal world, their actions are free in the noumenal world. For Habermas, morality arises from discourse, which is made necessary by their rationality and needs, rather than their freedom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pragmatic_ethics">Pragmatic ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Pragmatic ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pragmatic_ethics" title="Pragmatic ethics">Pragmatic ethics</a></div>
<p>Associated with the <a href="/wiki/Pragmatism" title="Pragmatism">pragmatists</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sanders_Peirce" title="Charles Sanders Peirce">Charles Sanders Peirce</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_James" title="William James">William James</a>, and especially <a href="/wiki/John_Dewey" title="John Dewey">John Dewey</a>, pragmatic ethics holds that moral correctness evolves similarly to scientific knowledge: socially over the course of many lifetimes. Thus, we should prioritize social reform over attempts to account for consequences, individual virtue or duty (although these may be worthwhile attempts, if social reform is provided for).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lafollette2000_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lafollette2000-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics_of_care">Ethics of care</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Ethics of care">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethics_of_care" title="Ethics of care">Ethics of care</a></div>
<p>Care ethics contrasts with more well-known ethical models, such as consequentialist theories (e.g. utilitarianism) and deontological theories (e.g., Kantian ethics) in that it seeks to incorporate traditionally feminized virtues and values that—proponents of care ethics contend—are absent in such traditional models of ethics. These values include the importance of empathetic relationships and compassion.
</p><p>Care-focused feminism is a branch of feminist thought, informed primarily by ethics of care as developed by <a href="/wiki/Carol_Gilligan" title="Carol Gilligan">Carol Gilligan</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GILLIGAN2009_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-GILLIGAN2009-57">[57]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Nel_Noddings" title="Nel Noddings">Nel Noddings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> This body of theory is critical of how caring is socially assigned to women, and consequently devalued. They write, "Care-focused feminists regard women's capacity for care as a human strength," that should be taught to and expected of men as well as women. Noddings proposes that ethical caring has the potential to be a more concrete evaluative model of moral dilemma than an ethic of justice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Noddings’ care-focused feminism requires practical application of <a href="/wiki/Nel_Noddings#Nel_Noddings'_relational_ethics" title="Nel Noddings">relational ethics</a>, predicated on an ethic of care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feminist_matrixial_ethics">Feminist matrixial ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Feminist matrixial ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Feminist_ethics" title="Feminist ethics">Feminist ethics</a></div>
<p>The 'metafeminist' theory of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matrixial_gaze" title="Matrixial gaze">matrixial gaze</a> and the matrixial<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> time-space, coined and developed <a href="/wiki/Bracha_L._Ettinger" title="Bracha L. Ettinger">Bracha L. Ettinger</a> since 1985,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> articulates a revolutionary philosophical approach that, in "daring to approach", to use <a href="/wiki/Griselda_Pollock" title="Griselda Pollock">Griselda Pollock</a>'s description of Ettinger's ethical turn,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> "the prenatal with the pre-maternal encounter", violence toward women at war, and the Shoah, has philosophically established the rights of each female subject over her own reproductive body, and offered a language to relate to human experiences which escape the phallic domain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The matrixial sphere is a psychic and symbolic dimension that the 'phallic' language and regulations cannot control. In Ettinger's model, the relations between self and other are of neither assimilation nor rejection but 'coemergence'. In her conversation with Emmanuel Levinas, 1991, Ettinger prooses that the source of human Ethics is feminine-maternal and feminine-pre-maternal matrixial encounter-event. Sexuality and maternality coexist and are not in contradiction (the contradiction established by <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Lacan" title="Jacques Lacan">Jacques Lacan</a>), and the feminine is not an absolute alterity (the alterity established by Jacques Lacan and <a href="/wiki/Emmanuel_Levinas" title="Emmanuel Levinas">Emmanuel Levinas</a>).
